					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Aircraft without any human pilot on board</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"UAV" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/UAV_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="UAV (disambiguation)">UAV (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"RPAS" redirects here; not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Rorschach_Performance_Assessment_System" title="Rorschach Performance Assessment System">Rorschach Performance Assessment System</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:MQ-9_Reaper_UAV_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/MQ-9_Reaper_UAV_%28cropped%29.jpg/500px-MQ-9_Reaper_UAV_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="340" height="223" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/MQ-9_Reaper_UAV_%28cropped%29.jpg/960px-MQ-9_Reaper_UAV_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3022" data-file-height="1986" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/General_Atomics_MQ-9_Reaper" title="General Atomics MQ-9 Reaper">General Atomics MQ-9 Reaper</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_combat_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned combat aerial vehicle">unmanned combat aerial vehicle</a> used for both <a href="/wiki/Intelligence,_surveillance,_target_acquisition,_and_reconnaissance" title="Intelligence, surveillance, target acquisition, and reconnaissance">intelligence, surveillance, target acquisition, and reconnaissance</a> (ISTAR) and drone strike missions.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:03_diha_gorsel_2020_02_12.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/03_diha_gorsel_2020_02_12.jpg/500px-03_diha_gorsel_2020_02_12.jpg" decoding="async" width="340" height="225" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/03_diha_gorsel_2020_02_12.jpg/960px-03_diha_gorsel_2020_02_12.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="7680" data-file-height="5089" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Baykar_Bayraktar_KALKAN" title="Baykar Bayraktar KALKAN">Baykar Bayraktar KALKAN</a> is a vertical take-off and landing (<a href="/wiki/VTOL" class="mw-redirect" title="VTOL">VTOL</a>) <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_surveillance_and_reconnaissance_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned surveillance and reconnaissance aerial vehicle">unmanned surveillance and reconnaissance aerial vehicle</a> capable of operating from <a href="/wiki/Drone_carrier" title="Drone carrier">drone carrier</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:PIA22460-Mars2020Mission-Helicopter-20180525_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/PIA22460-Mars2020Mission-Helicopter-20180525_%28cropped%29.jpg/500px-PIA22460-Mars2020Mission-Helicopter-20180525_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="340" height="219" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/PIA22460-Mars2020Mission-Helicopter-20180525_%28cropped%29.jpg/960px-PIA22460-Mars2020Mission-Helicopter-20180525_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2181" data-file-height="1404" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Ingenuity_(helicopter)" title="Ingenuity (helicopter)">Ingenuity</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_sky" title="Extraterrestrial sky">extraterrestrial</a> <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_robot" title="Autonomous robot">autonomous</a> UAV  that performed first powered controlled flight by an aircraft on another planet.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Spying_quadcopter_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Spying_quadcopter_%28cropped%29.jpg/500px-Spying_quadcopter_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="340" height="254" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Spying_quadcopter_%28cropped%29.jpg/960px-Spying_quadcopter_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1981" data-file-height="1481" /></a><figcaption>Small civilian <a href="/wiki/Quadcopter" title="Quadcopter">quadcopter</a> UAVs such as <a href="/wiki/DJI_(company)" class="mw-redirect" title="DJI (company)">DJI</a> <a href="/wiki/Phantom_(UAV)" class="mw-redirect" title="Phantom (UAV)">Phantom</a> designed for commercial and recreational <a href="/wiki/Aerial_photography" title="Aerial photography">aerial photography</a>, which are also useful for short-range reconnaissance and strike (if converted) missions by the law enforcement forces and the military.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Schiebel_CAMCOPTER_S-100_7.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Schiebel_CAMCOPTER_S-100_7.jpg/500px-Schiebel_CAMCOPTER_S-100_7.jpg" decoding="async" width="340" height="227" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Schiebel_CAMCOPTER_S-100_7.jpg/960px-Schiebel_CAMCOPTER_S-100_7.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3048" data-file-height="2032" /></a><figcaption>Although most large military UAVs are <a href="/wiki/Fixed-wing_aircraft" title="Fixed-wing aircraft">fixed-wing aircraft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rotorcraft" title="Rotorcraft">rotorcraft</a> designs (i.e., RUAVs) such as this <a href="/wiki/Schiebel_Camcopter_S-100" title="Schiebel Camcopter S-100">Schiebel Camcopter S-100</a> are also used.</figcaption></figure>
<p>An <b>unmanned aerial vehicle</b> (<b>UAV</b>) or <b>unmanned aircraft system</b> (<b>UAS</b>), commonly known as a <b>drone</b>, is an <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a> with no human <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_pilot" title="Aircraft pilot">pilot</a>, crew, or passengers on board, but rather is controlled remotely or is autonomous.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto1&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-uav&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uav-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> UAVs were originally developed through the twentieth century for military missions too "dull, dirty or dangerous"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> for humans, and by the twenty-first had become essential assets to most militaries. As control technologies improved and costs fell, their use expanded to many non-military applications.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These include <a href="/wiki/Aerial_photography" title="Aerial photography">aerial photography</a>, area coverage,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tits&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tits-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_drone" title="Agricultural drone">precision agriculture</a>, forest fire monitoring,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ast&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ast-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> river monitoring,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Manfreda&#95;Dor&#95;Remote&#95;sensing&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manfreda_Dor_Remote_sensing-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Environmental_monitoring" title="Environmental monitoring">environmental monitoring</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Weather_drone" title="Weather drone">weather observation</a>, policing and surveillance, infrastructure inspections, smuggling,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Delivery_drone" title="Delivery drone">product deliveries</a>, entertainment and <a href="/wiki/Drone_racing" title="Drone racing">drone racing</a>.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" /></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Terminology">Terminology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many terms are used for aircraft which fly without any persons on board.
</p><p>An <b>unmanned aerial vehicle</b> (<b>UAV</b>) is defined as a "powered, aerial vehicle that does not carry a human operator, uses <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamic_force" title="Aerodynamic force">aerodynamic forces</a> to provide vehicle lift, can fly autonomously or be piloted remotely, can be expendable or recoverable, and can carry a lethal or nonlethal payload".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> UAV is a term that is commonly applied to military use cases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Missile" title="Missile">Missiles</a> with warheads are generally not considered UAVs because the vehicle itself is a munition, but certain types of propeller-based missile are often called "<a href="/wiki/Loitering_munition" title="Loitering munition">kamikaze drones</a>" by the public and media. Also, the relation of UAVs to <a href="/wiki/Radio-controlled_aircraft" title="Radio-controlled aircraft">remote controlled model aircraft</a> is unclear in some jurisdictions. The US FAA now defines any unmanned flying craft as a UAV regardless of weight.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Similar terms are <i><b>remotely piloted aircraft</b></i> (<b>RPA</b>) and <i><b>remotely piloted aerial vehicle</b></i> (<b>RPAV</b>).
</p><p>UAVs or RPAVs can also be seen as a component of an <b>unmanned aircraft system</b> (<b>UAS</b>), which also includes a ground-based controller and a system of communications with the aircraft.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ast&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ast-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
The term UAS was adopted by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">United States Department of Defense</a> (DoD) and the United States <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> (FAA) in 2005 according to their Unmanned Aircraft System Roadmap 2005–2030.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization" title="International Civil Aviation Organization">International Civil Aviation Organization</a> (ICAO) and the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Authority_(United_Kingdom)" title="Civil Aviation Authority (United Kingdom)">British Civil Aviation Authority</a> adopted this term, also used in the European Union's <a href="/wiki/SESAR_Joint_Undertaking" title="SESAR Joint Undertaking">Single European Sky (SES) Air Traffic Management (ATM) Research</a> (SESAR Joint Undertaking) roadmap for 2020.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This term emphasizes the importance of elements other than the aircraft. It includes elements such as ground control stations, data links and other support equipment. Similar terms are <i><b>unmanned aircraft vehicle system</b></i> (<b>UAVS</b>) and <i><b>remotely piloted aircraft system</b></i> (<b>RPAS</b>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many similar terms are in use. Under new regulations which came into effect 1 June 2019, the term RPAS has been adopted by the Canadian government to mean "a set of configurable elements consisting of a remotely piloted aircraft, its control station, the command and control links and any other system elements required during flight operation".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In common usage, "drone" is often applied to both military and civilian UAVs, while technical and regulatory documents may prefer terms such as UAV, UAS, RPAS, or uncrewed aircraft. The term <b>drone</b> has been used from the early days of <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation</a>, some being applied to remotely flown target <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a> used for practice firing of a battleship's guns, such as the 1920s <a href="/wiki/Fairey_III" title="Fairey III">Fairey Queen</a> biplane <a href="/wiki/Floatplane" class="mw-redirect" title="Floatplane">floatplane</a> and 1930s <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland_Tiger_Moth#Gunnery_target_drone" title="De Havilland Tiger Moth">de Havilland Queen Bee</a> biplane. Later examples included the <a href="/wiki/Airspeed_Queen_Wasp" title="Airspeed Queen Wasp">Airspeed Queen Wasp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miles_Martinet" title="Miles Martinet">Miles Queen Martinet</a>, before ultimate replacement by the <a href="/wiki/GAF_Jindivik" title="GAF Jindivik">GAF Jindivik</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The term remains in common use. In addition to the software, autonomous drones also employ a host of advanced technologies that allow them to carry out their missions without human intervention, such as cloud computing, computer vision, artificial intelligence, machine learning, deep learning, and thermal sensors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For recreational uses, an <a href="/wiki/Aerial_photography" title="Aerial photography">aerial photography</a> drone is an aircraft that has first-person video, autonomous capabilities, or both.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dronesetc&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dronesetc-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Uncrewed_vehicle" title="Uncrewed vehicle">Uncrewed</a>" is sometimes used rather than "unmanned" when referring to UAVs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Classification_types">Classification types</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Classification types"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>UAVs may be classified like any other <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a>, according to design configuration such as weight or engine type, maximum flight altitude, degree of operational autonomy, operational role, etc. According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">United States Department of Defense</a>, UAVs are classified into five categories below:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-uavclassificationA&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uavclassificationA-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-uavclassificationB&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uavclassificationB-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align:right;">

<p>Other classifications of UAVs include:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-uavclassificationA&#95;27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uavclassificationA-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Range_and_endurance">Range and endurance</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Range and endurance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There are usually five categories when UAVs are classified by range and endurance:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-uavclassificationA&#95;27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uavclassificationA-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

</td>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Size">Size</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Size"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There are usually four categories when UAVs are classified by size, with at least one of the dimensions (length or wingspan) meet the following respective limits:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-uavclassificationA&#95;27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uavclassificationA-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Weight">Weight</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Weight"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Based on their weight, drones can be classified into five categories:
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align:center;">

<p>NATO uses a similar classification shown below:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto1&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:UASclassification.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/UASclassification.png/250px-UASclassification.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="214" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/UASclassification.png/500px-UASclassification.png 1.5x" data-file-width="1346" data-file-height="1153" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> classification of <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_Aerial_Vehicles" class="mw-redirect" title="Unmanned Aerial Vehicles">Unmanned Aerial Vehicles</a></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Degree_of_autonomy">Degree of autonomy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Degree of autonomy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Drones can also be classified based on the degree of autonomy in their flight operations. ICAO classifies unmanned aircraft as either remotely piloted aircraft or fully autonomous.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Percepto&#95;Drones&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Percepto_Drones-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some UAVs offer intermediate degrees of autonomy. For example, a vehicle may be remotely piloted in most contexts but have an autonomous return-to-base operation. Some aircraft types may optionally fly manned or as UAVs, which may include manned aircraft transformed into manned or optionally piloted UAVs (OPVs). The flight of UAVs may operate under remote control by a human operator, as <b>remotely piloted aircraft</b> (<b>RPA</b>), or with various degrees of <a href="/wiki/Vehicular_automation" title="Vehicular automation">autonomy</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Autopilot" title="Autopilot">autopilot</a> assistance, up to fully autonomous aircraft that have no provision for human intervention.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ICAO&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICAO-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Altitude">Altitude</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Altitude"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Based on the altitude, the following UAV classifications have been used at industry events such as <a href="/wiki/ParcAberporth" title="ParcAberporth">ParcAberporth</a> Unmanned Systems forum:
</p>
<ul><li>Hand-held 2,000&#160;ft (600&#160;m) altitude, about 2&#160;km range</li>
<li>Close 5,000&#160;ft (1,500&#160;m) altitude, up to 10&#160;km range</li>
<li>NATO type 10,000&#160;ft (3,000&#160;m) altitude, up to 50&#160;km range</li>
<li>Tactical 18,000&#160;ft (5,500&#160;m) altitude, about 160&#160;km range</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Medium-altitude_long-endurance_unmanned_aerial_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Medium-altitude long-endurance unmanned aerial vehicle">MALE (medium altitude, long endurance)</a> up to 30,000&#160;ft (9,000&#160;m) and range over 200&#160;km</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/High-Altitude_Long_Endurance" class="mw-redirect" title="High-Altitude Long Endurance">HALE (high altitude, long endurance)</a> over 30,000&#160;ft (9,100&#160;m) and indefinite range</li>
<li>Hypersonic high-speed, supersonic (Mach 1–5) or hypersonic (Mach 5+) 50,000&#160;ft (15,200&#160;m) or suborbital altitude, range over 200&#160;km</li>
<li>Orbital low Earth orbit (Mach 25+)</li>
<li>CIS lunar Earth-Moon transfer</li>
<li>Computer-assisted carrier guidance system (CACGS) for UAVs</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Composite_criteria">Composite criteria</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Composite criteria"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An example of classification based on the composite criteria is U.S. Military's <a href="/wiki/U.S._military_UAS_groups" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. military UAS groups">unmanned aerial systems</a> (UAS) classification of UAVs based on weight, maximum altitude and speed of the UAV component.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Lift_type">Lift type</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Lift type"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="FFLO">FFLO</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: FFLO"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><i>Forward flight lift only</i> classified by  <a href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization" title="International Civil Aviation Organization">ICAO</a> with the aircraft type designator FFLO - UAV refers to plane-like drones 
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="VFHC">VFHC</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: VFHC"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><i>Vertical flight/hover capability</i> classified by  <a href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization" title="International Civil Aviation Organization">ICAO</a> with the aircraft type designator VFHC - UAV refers to drones using rotaing blades for lift as well as motion
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Power_sources">Power sources</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Power sources"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>UAVs can be classified based on their power or energy source, which significantly impacts their flight duration, range, and environmental impact. The main categories include:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Battery-powered (electric):</b> These UAVs use rechargeable batteries, offering quiet operation and lower maintenance but potentially limited flight times. The reduced noise levels make them suitable for urban environments and sensitive operations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Fuel-powered (internal combustion):</b> Utilizing traditional fuels like gasoline or diesel, these UAVs often have longer flight times but may be noisier and require more maintenance. They are typically used for applications requiring extended endurance or heavy payload capacity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Hybrid:</b> Combining electric and fuel power sources, hybrid UAVs aim to balance the benefits of both systems for improved performance and efficiency. This configuration could allow for versatility in mission profiles and adaptability to different operational requirements.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Hydrogen fuel cell:</b> <a href="/wiki/Proton-exchange_membrane_fuel_cell" title="Proton-exchange membrane fuel cell">hydrogen fuel cells</a> offer the potential for longer flight times than batteries yet stealthier (no heat signature) operation than combustion engines.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto3&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The high energy density of hydrogen makes it a promising option for future UAV propulsion systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Solar-powered:</b> Equipped with solar panels, these UAVs can potentially achieve extended flight times by harnessing solar energy, especially at high altitudes. Solar-powered UAVs may be particularly suited for long-endurance missions and environmental monitoring applications.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Nuclear-powered:</b> While nuclear power has been explored for larger aircraft, its application in UAVs remains largely theoretical due to safety concerns and regulatory challenges. Research in this area is ongoing but faces significant hurdles before practical implementation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_unmanned_aerial_vehicles" title="History of unmanned aerial vehicles">History of unmanned aerial vehicles</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Winston_Churchill_and_the_Secretary_of_State_for_War_waiting_to_see_the_launch_of_a_de_Havilland_Queen_Bee_radio-controlled_target_drone,_6_June_1941._H10307.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Winston_Churchill_and_the_Secretary_of_State_for_War_waiting_to_see_the_launch_of_a_de_Havilland_Queen_Bee_radio-controlled_target_drone%2C_6_June_1941._H10307.jpg/250px-thumbnail.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="183" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Winston_Churchill_and_the_Secretary_of_State_for_War_waiting_to_see_the_launch_of_a_de_Havilland_Queen_Bee_radio-controlled_target_drone%2C_6_June_1941._H10307.jpg/500px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2415" data-file-height="1772" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> and others waiting to watch the launch of a <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland_Tiger_Moth#Gunnery_target_drone" title="De Havilland Tiger Moth">de Havilland Queen Bee</a> <a href="/wiki/Target_drone" title="Target drone">target drone</a>, 6 June 1941</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Teledyne-Ryan-Firebee-hatzerim-1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Teledyne-Ryan-Firebee-hatzerim-1.jpg/250px-Teledyne-Ryan-Firebee-hatzerim-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="159" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Teledyne-Ryan-Firebee-hatzerim-1.jpg/500px-Teledyne-Ryan-Firebee-hatzerim-1.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1135" data-file-height="724" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Firebee" title="Ryan Firebee">Ryan Firebee</a>, one of a series of target drones/unpiloted aerial vehicles that first flew in 1951. <a href="/wiki/Israeli_Air_Force_Museum" title="Israeli Air Force Museum">Israeli Air Force Museum</a>, Hatzerim airbase, Israel, 2006</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Last_preparation_before_first_tactical_mission_across_Suez_canal_(1969).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Last_preparation_before_first_tactical_mission_across_Suez_canal_%281969%29.jpg/250px-Last_preparation_before_first_tactical_mission_across_Suez_canal_%281969%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="163" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Last_preparation_before_first_tactical_mission_across_Suez_canal_%281969%29.jpg/500px-Last_preparation_before_first_tactical_mission_across_Suez_canal_%281969%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1037" data-file-height="678" /></a><figcaption>Last preparations before the first tactical UAV mission across the Suez Canal (1969). Standing: Major Shabtai Brill from the Israeli Intelligence Corps, the innovator of the tactical UAV.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Tadiran-Mastiff-III-hatzerim-1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Tadiran-Mastiff-III-hatzerim-1.jpg/250px-Tadiran-Mastiff-III-hatzerim-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="141" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Tadiran-Mastiff-III-hatzerim-1.jpg/500px-Tadiran-Mastiff-III-hatzerim-1.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1295" data-file-height="732" /></a><figcaption>The Israeli <a href="/wiki/Tadiran_Mastiff" title="Tadiran Mastiff">Tadiran Mastiff</a>, which first flew in 1975, is seen by many as the first modern battlefield UAV, due to its data-link system, endurance-loitering, and live video-streaming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="1800's"><span id="1800.27s"></span>1800's</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 1800&#039;s"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Early_drones">Early drones</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Early drones"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The earliest recorded use of an unmanned aerial vehicle for warfighting occurred in July 1849,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with a <a href="/wiki/Balloon_carrier" title="Balloon carrier">balloon carrier</a> (the precursor to the <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a>)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kaplan&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in the first offensive use of <a href="/wiki/Air_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Air power">air power</a> in <a href="/wiki/Naval_aviation" title="Naval aviation">naval aviation</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hallion&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hallion-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-LaymanFirst&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaymanFirst-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-RennerFirst&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RennerFirst-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Austrian forces besieging Venice attempted to launch some 200 <a href="/wiki/Incendiary_balloon" title="Incendiary balloon">incendiary balloons</a> at the besieged city. The balloons were launched mainly from land; however, some were also launched from the Austrian ship <a href="/wiki/SMS_Vulcano" title="SMS Vulcano">SMS&#160;<i>Vulcano</i></a>. At least one bomb fell in the city; however, due to the wind changing after launch, most of the balloons missed their target, and some drifted back over Austrian lines and the launching ship <i>Vulcano</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Murphy&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Haydon&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haydon-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mikesh&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikesh-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Spanish engineer <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Torres_Quevedo" title="Leonardo Torres Quevedo">Leonardo Torres Quevedo</a> introduced a radio-based control-system called the <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Torres_Quevedo#Radio_control:_the_Telekino" title="Leonardo Torres Quevedo"><i>Telekino</i></a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> at the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Academy_of_Science" class="mw-redirect" title="Paris Academy of Science">Paris Academy of Science</a> in 1903, as a way of testing <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airships</a> without risking human life.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="1900's"><span id="1900.27s"></span>1900's</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 1900&#039;s"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Significant development of drones started in the 1900s, and originally focused on providing practice targets for training <a href="/wiki/Military_personnel" title="Military personnel">military personnel</a>. The earliest attempt at a powered UAV was <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Low" title="Archibald Low">A. M. Low</a>'s "Aerial Target" in 1916.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-autogenerated1&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Low confirmed that Geoffrey de Havilland's monoplane was the one that flew under control on 21 March 1917 using his radio system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Following this successful demonstration in the spring of 1917 Low was transferred to develop aircraft controlled fast motor launches <a href="/wiki/Coastal_Motor_Boat" class="mw-redirect" title="Coastal Motor Boat">D.C.B.s</a> with the Royal Navy in 1918 intended to attack shipping and port installations and he also assisted <a href="/wiki/Frank_Arthur_Brock" title="Frank Arthur Brock">Wing Commander Brock</a> in preparations for the <a href="/wiki/Zeebrugge_Raid" title="Zeebrugge Raid">Zeebrugge Raid</a>. Other <a href="/wiki/British_unmanned_aerial_vehicles_of_World_War_I" title="British unmanned aerial vehicles of World War I">British unmanned developments</a> followed, leading to the fleet of over 400 <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland_Tiger_Moth" title="De Havilland Tiger Moth">de Havilland 82 Queen Bee</a> aerial targets that went into service in 1935.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla</a> described a fleet of uncrewed aerial combat vehicles in 1915.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-armyeyes&#95;55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armyeyes-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These developments also inspired the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Kettering_Bug" title="Kettering Bug">Kettering Bug</a> by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Kettering" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Kettering">Charles Kettering</a> from <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">Dayton, Ohio</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hewitt-Sperry_Automatic_Airplane" class="mw-redirect" title="Hewitt-Sperry Automatic Airplane">Hewitt-Sperry Automatic Airplane</a> – initially meant as an uncrewed plane that would carry an explosive payload to a predetermined target. Development continued during World War I, when the <a href="/wiki/Dayton-Wright_Airplane_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Dayton-Wright Airplane Company">Dayton-Wright Airplane Company</a> invented a pilotless <a href="/wiki/Aerial_torpedo" title="Aerial torpedo">aerial torpedo</a> that would explode at a preset time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The film star and <a href="/wiki/Model-airplane" class="mw-redirect" title="Model-airplane">model-airplane</a> enthusiast <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Denny_(actor)" title="Reginald Denny (actor)">Reginald Denny</a> developed the first scaled remote piloted vehicle in 1935.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-autogenerated1&#95;53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Soviet researchers experimented with controlling <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_TB-1" title="Tupolev TB-1">Tupolev TB-1</a> bombers remotely in the late 1930s.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="World_War_II">World War II</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: World War II"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 1940, Reginald Denny started the <a href="/wiki/Radioplane_Company" title="Radioplane Company">Radioplane Company</a> and more models emerged during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>&#160;&#8211;&#32; used both to train antiaircraft gunners and to fly attack-missions. <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> produced and used various UAV aircraft during the war, like the <a href="/wiki/Argus_As_292" title="Argus As 292">Argus As 292</a> and the <a href="/wiki/V-1_flying_bomb" title="V-1 flying bomb">V-1 flying bomb</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Jet_engine" title="Jet engine">jet engine</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fascist_Italy_(1922%E2%80%931943)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fascist Italy (1922–1943)">Fascist Italy</a> developed a specialised drone version of the <a href="/wiki/Savoia-Marchetti_SM.79_Sparviero" title="Savoia-Marchetti SM.79 Sparviero">Savoia-Marchetti SM.79</a> flown by remote control, although the Armistice with Italy was enacted prior to any operational deployment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Postwar_period">Postwar period</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Postwar period"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>After World War II development continued in vehicles such as the American <a href="/wiki/JB-4" title="JB-4">JB-4</a> (using television/radio-command guidance), the Australian <a href="/wiki/GAF_Jindivik" title="GAF Jindivik">GAF Jindivik</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Aeronautical" title="Ryan Aeronautical">Teledyne Ryan</a> <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Firebee" title="Ryan Firebee">Firebee I</a> of 1951, while companies like <a href="/wiki/Beechcraft" title="Beechcraft">Beechcraft</a> offered their <a href="/wiki/Beechcraft_MQM-61_Cardinal" title="Beechcraft MQM-61 Cardinal">Model 1001</a> for the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Navy">U.S. Navy</a> in 1955.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-autogenerated1&#95;53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, they were little more than remote-controlled airplanes until the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>. In 1959, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a>, concerned about losing pilots over hostile territory, began planning for the use of uncrewed aircraft.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWagner1982xi&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner1982xi-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Planning intensified after the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> <a href="/wiki/1960_U-2_incident" title="1960 U-2 incident">shot down a U-2</a> in 1960. Within days, a highly <a href="/wiki/Classified_information" title="Classified information">classified</a> UAV program started under the code name of "Red Wagon".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWagner1982xi,&#95;xii&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner1982xi,_xii-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The August 1964 <a href="/wiki/Tonkin_Gulf_Incident" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonkin Gulf Incident">clash in the Tonkin Gulf</a> between naval units of the U.S. and the <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnamese_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="North Vietnamese Navy">North Vietnamese Navy</a> initiated America's highly classified UAVs (<a href="/wiki/Ryan_Model_147" title="Ryan Model 147">Ryan Model 147</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ryan_AQM-91_Firefly" title="Ryan AQM-91 Firefly">Ryan AQM-91 Firefly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_D-21" title="Lockheed D-21">Lockheed D-21</a>) into their first combat missions of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWagner1982xii&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner1982xii-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When the Chinese government<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWagner198279&#95;62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner198279-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> showed photographs of downed U.S. UAVs via <i>Wide World Photos</i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWagner198278,&#95;79&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner198278,_79-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the official U.S. response was "no comment".
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/War_of_Attrition" title="War of Attrition">War of Attrition</a> (1967–1970) in the Middle East, Israeli intelligence tested the first tactical UAVs installed with <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a> cameras, which successfully returned photos from across the Suez Canal. This was the first time that tactical UAVs that could be launched and landed on any short runway (unlike the heavier jet-based UAVs) were developed and tested in battle.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1973 <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Yom Kippur War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> used UAVs as decoys to spur opposing forces into wasting expensive anti-aircraft missiles.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After the 1973 Yom Kippur War, a few key people from the team that developed this early UAV joined a small startup company that aimed to develop UAVs into a commercial product, eventually purchased by Tadiran and leading to the development of the first Israeli UAV.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears (January 2019)">pages&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In 1973, the U.S. military officially confirmed that they had been using UAVs in Southeast Asia (Vietnam).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWagner1982202&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner1982202-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Over 5,000 U.S. airmen had been killed and over 1,000 more were <a href="/wiki/Missing_In_Action" class="mw-redirect" title="Missing In Action">missing</a> or <a href="/wiki/POW" class="mw-redirect" title="POW">captured</a>. The USAF <a href="/wiki/100th_Strategic_Reconnaissance_Wing" class="mw-redirect" title="100th Strategic Reconnaissance Wing">100th Strategic Reconnaissance Wing</a> flew about 3,435 UAV missions during the war<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWagner1982200,&#95;212&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner1982200,_212-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> at a cost of about 554 UAVs lost to all causes. In the words of USAF <a href="/wiki/General" class="mw-redirect" title="General">General</a> <a href="/wiki/George_S._Brown" class="mw-redirect" title="George S. Brown">George S. Brown</a>, Commander, <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Systems_Command" title="Air Force Systems Command">Air Force Systems Command</a>, in 1972, "The only reason we need (UAVs) is that we don't want to needlessly expend the man in the cockpit."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWagner1982208&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner1982208-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Later that year, General <a href="/wiki/John_C._Meyer" title="John C. Meyer">John C. Meyer</a>, Commander in Chief, <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Air_Command" title="Strategic Air Command">Strategic Air Command</a>, stated, "we let the drone do the high-risk flying&#160;... the loss rate is high, but we are willing to risk more of them&#160;...they save lives!"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWagner1982208&#95;69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner1982208-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1973 <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Yom Kippur War</a>, Soviet-supplied <a href="/wiki/Surface-to-air_missile" title="Surface-to-air missile">surface-to-air missile</a>-batteries in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> caused heavy damage to Israeli <a href="/wiki/Fighter_jet" class="mw-redirect" title="Fighter jet">fighter jets</a>. As a result, Israel developed the <a href="/wiki/IAI_Scout" title="IAI Scout">IAI Scout</a> as the first UAV with real-time surveillance.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The images and radar decoys provided by these UAVs helped Israel to <a href="/wiki/Operation_Mole_Cricket_19" title="Operation Mole Cricket 19">completely neutralize</a> the Syrian <a href="/wiki/Air_defense" class="mw-redirect" title="Air defense">air defenses</a> at the start of the <a href="/wiki/1982_Lebanon_War" title="1982 Lebanon War">1982 Lebanon War</a>, resulting in no pilots downed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WSJLevinson&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJLevinson-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Israel in 1987, UAVs were first used as proof-of-concept of super-agility, post-stall controlled flight in combat-flight simulations that involved tailless, stealth-technology-based, three-dimensional thrust vectoring flight-control, and jet-steering.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Post-Cold_War_UAVs">Post-Cold War UAVs</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Post-Cold War UAVs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:STM_Kargu.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/STM_Kargu.png/250px-STM_Kargu.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="141" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/STM_Kargu.png/500px-STM_Kargu.png 1.5x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="675" /></a><figcaption>The Turkish <a href="/wiki/STM_Kargu" title="STM Kargu">STM Kargu</a> was the first <a href="/wiki/Lethal_autonomous_weapon" title="Lethal autonomous weapon">lethal autonomous weapon</a> to attack enemy combatants in warfare.</figcaption></figure>
<p>With the maturing and miniaturization of applicable technologies in the 1980s and 1990s, interest in UAVs grew within the higher echelons of the U.S. military. The U.S. funded the <a href="/wiki/Counterterrorism_Mission_Center" title="Counterterrorism Mission Center">Counterterrorism Center</a> (CTC) within the CIA, which sought to fight terrorism with the aid of modernized drone technology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the 1990s, the U.S. DoD gave a contract to <a href="/wiki/AAI_Corporation" title="AAI Corporation">AAI Corporation</a> along with Israeli company Malat. The U.S. Navy bought the <a href="/wiki/AAI_RQ-2_Pioneer" title="AAI RQ-2 Pioneer">AAI Pioneer UAV</a> that AAI and Malat developed jointly. Many of these UAVs saw service in the <a href="/wiki/1991_Gulf_War" class="mw-redirect" title="1991 Gulf War">1991 Gulf War</a>. UAVs demonstrated the possibility of cheaper, more capable fighting-machines, deployable without risk to aircrews. Initial generations primarily involved <a href="/wiki/Surveillance_aircraft" title="Surveillance aircraft">surveillance aircraft</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_combat_air_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Unmanned combat air vehicle">some carried armaments</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/General_Atomics_MQ-1_Predator" title="General Atomics MQ-1 Predator">General Atomics MQ-1 Predator</a>, that launched <a href="/wiki/AGM-114_Hellfire" title="AGM-114 Hellfire">AGM-114 Hellfire</a> <a href="/wiki/Air-to-ground_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Air-to-ground missile">air-to-ground missiles</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="2000s">2000s</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 2000s"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/CAPECON_project" title="CAPECON project">CAPECON</a>, a <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> <a href="/wiki/Project" title="Project">project</a> to develop UAVs,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Z.&#95;Goraj,&#95;A.&#95;Frydrychewicz,&#95;R.&#95;Świtkiewicz,&#95;B.&#95;Hernik,&#95;J.&#95;Gadomski,&#95;T.&#95;Goetzendorf-Grabowski,&#95;M.&#95;Figat,&#95;St&#95;Suchodolski,&#95;W.&#95;Chajec&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Z._Goraj,_A._Frydrychewicz,_R._Świtkiewicz,_B._Hernik,_J._Gadomski,_T._Goetzendorf-Grabowski,_M._Figat,_St_Suchodolski,_W._Chajec-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> ran from 1 May 2002 to 31 December 2005.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CORDIS&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CORDIS-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> (USAF) employed 7,494 UAVs&#160;&#8211;&#32; almost one in three USAF aircraft.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WIRED&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WIRED-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Singer&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singer-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> <a href="/wiki/Use_of_UAVs_by_the_CIA" class="mw-redirect" title="Use of UAVs by the CIA">also operated UAVs</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 2013 at least 50 countries used UAVs. China, Iran, Israel, Pakistan, Turkey, and others designed and built their own varieties. The use of drones has continued to increase.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sayler&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sayler-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to their wide proliferation, no comprehensive list of UAV systems exists.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Singer&#95;79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singer-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Franke&#95;82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Franke-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, the United States Federal Aviation Administration (FAA) allowed the usage of unmanned aerial vehicles inside civilian airspace with specific regulations, laying out legal groundwork for consumer drone usage inside the United States.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, <a href="/wiki/DJI_(company)" class="mw-redirect" title="DJI (company)">DJI</a> released the first model of the <a href="/wiki/Phantom_(UAV)" class="mw-redirect" title="Phantom (UAV)">Phantom</a> drone, DJI's fully assembled drone model. Priced at $629, Phantom was an entry-level drone, featuring much more user-friendly experiences than other drones on the market at the time. The DJI Phantom was considered one of the most influential consumer drone products ever made. With its affordability, accessibility, and user-friendly software, it quickly captured the consumer drone market from hobbyists, professionals, and introduced the modern aerial photography drone form factor to the general public.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 2017, DJI alone had over 75% of the global consumer drone market share.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-cnbc&#95;1709&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc_1709-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, a <a href="/wiki/STM_Kargu" title="STM Kargu">Kargu 2</a> drone hunted down and attacked a human target in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, according to a report from the <a href="/wiki/UN_Security_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Security Council">UN Security Council</a>'s Panel of Experts on Libya, published in March 2021. This may have been the first time an <a href="/wiki/Lethal_autonomous_weapon" title="Lethal autonomous weapon">autonomous killer-robot</a> armed with lethal weaponry attacked human beings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Superior drone technology, specifically the Turkish <a href="/wiki/Bayraktar_TB2" class="mw-redirect" title="Bayraktar TB2">Bayraktar TB2</a>, played a role in Azerbaijan's successes in the <a href="/wiki/2020_Nagorno-Karabakh_war" class="mw-redirect" title="2020 Nagorno-Karabakh war">2020 Nagorno-Karabakh war</a> against Armenia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Helicopter_Above_Perseverance_on_Mars_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Helicopter_Above_Perseverance_on_Mars_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Helicopter_Above_Perseverance_on_Mars_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="163" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Helicopter_Above_Perseverance_on_Mars_%28cropped%29.jpg/500px-Helicopter_Above_Perseverance_on_Mars_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="624" data-file-height="408" /></a><figcaption>Artist's concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Ingenuity_(helicopter)" title="Ingenuity (helicopter)">Ingenuity</a></i> landing on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>UAVs are also used in <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> missions. The <i><a href="/wiki/Ingenuity_(helicopter)" title="Ingenuity (helicopter)">Ingenuity</a></i> helicopter is an autonomous UAV that operated on Mars from 2021 to 2024. As of 2024<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly_(Titan_space_probe)" title="Dragonfly (Titan space probe)">Dragonfly spacecraft</a> is being developed and is aiming to reach and examine <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>'s moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>. Its primary goal is to roam around the surface, expanding the amount of area to be researched previously seen by <a href="/wiki/Lander_(spacecraft)" title="Lander (spacecraft)">landers</a>. As a UAV, Dragonfly allows examination of potentially diverse types of soil. The drone is set to launch in 2027, and is estimated to take seven more years to reach the Saturnian system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Miniaturization is also supporting the development of small UAVs, which can be used as an individual system or in a fleet, offering the possibility to survey large areas in a relatively small amount of time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/April_2024_Iranian_strikes_against_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="April 2024 Iranian strikes against Israel">April 2024 Iranian strikes against Israel</a> took place on 13 April 2024 when the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Revolutionary_Guard_Corps" title="Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps">Iranian Revolutionary Guard</a> and other groups of the <a href="/wiki/Axis_of_Resistance" title="Axis of Resistance">Axis of Resistance</a> launched about 300 drones at <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, a distance of about 1,500 kilometers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:02&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to data from <a href="/wiki/GlobalData" title="GlobalData">GlobalData</a>, the global military uncrewed aerial systems (UAS) market, which forms a significant part of the UAV industry, is projected to experience a compound annual growth rate of 4.8% over the next decade. This represents a near doubling in market size, from $12.5 billion in 2024 to an estimated $20 billion by 2034.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Russo-Ukrainian_war">Russo-Ukrainian war</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Russo-Ukrainian war"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:UA_DPSU_Mavic_operators_01.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/UA_DPSU_Mavic_operators_01.jpg/250px-UA_DPSU_Mavic_operators_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/UA_DPSU_Mavic_operators_01.jpg/500px-UA_DPSU_Mavic_operators_01.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="999" /></a><figcaption>Ukrainian drone operator controlling a <a href="/wiki/DJI_Mavic" title="DJI Mavic">DJI Mavic</a> drone</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian invasion of Ukraine">Russian invasion of Ukraine</a> in 2022 was widely described as the first full-scale war featuring the large-scale use of commercial and consumer-grade UAVs in military settings, particularly <a href="/wiki/Quadcopters" class="mw-redirect" title="Quadcopters">quadcopters</a> and <a href="/wiki/First-person_view_(radio_control)" class="mw-redirect" title="First-person view (radio control)">first-person view</a> (FPV) drones, modified with sensors and explosives for various missions and tactical usage. The cheap and widely available consumer drone and small UAVs have dramatically shifted modern warfare and given rise to new offensive and defensive strategies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Both Ukraine and Russia used small consumer drones extensively during the war, which served as a tactical surveillance, strike, and propaganda tools.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dji&#95;usage&#95;uk&#95;98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dji_usage_uk-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Consumer drones were sourced by governments, hobbyists, international donations to Ukraine and Russia to support each side on the battlefield, and were often flown by drone hobbyists recruited by the armed forces.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-fp&#95;2302&#95;99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fp_2302-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Consumer drones' affordability, high performance, and reliability contributed to their popularity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:20&#95;100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:20-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-fp&#95;2302&#95;99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fp_2302-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They were also preferred for their commercial availability.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Companies attempted to restrict military usage of consumer product, but effect was limited,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as donors and buyers could ship the drones across the border via intermediates and modified their software to circumnavigate the restrictions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Ukraine, small commercial UAVs and FPV drones had become an ubiquitous and critical part of the war.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Design">Design</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Design"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:UAV_Physical_and_hardware.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/UAV_Physical_and_hardware.jpg/250px-UAV_Physical_and_hardware.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="157" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/UAV_Physical_and_hardware.jpg/500px-UAV_Physical_and_hardware.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="838" data-file-height="525" /></a><figcaption>General physical structure of a UAV</figcaption></figure>
<p>Crewed and uncrewed aircraft of the same type generally have recognizably similar physical components. The main exceptions are the <a href="/wiki/Cockpit" title="Cockpit">cockpit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_control_system_(aircraft)" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental control system (aircraft)">environmental control system</a> or <a href="/wiki/Life-support_system" title="Life-support system">life-support systems</a>. Some UAVs carry payloads (such as a camera) that weigh considerably less than an adult human, and as a result, can be considerably smaller. Though they carry heavy payloads, weaponized military UAVs are lighter than their crewed counterparts with comparable armaments.
</p><p>Small civilian UAVs have no <a href="/wiki/Life-critical_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Life-critical system">life-critical systems</a>, and can thus be built out of lighter but less sturdy materials and shapes, and can use less robustly tested electronic control systems. For small UAVs, the <a href="/wiki/Quadcopter" title="Quadcopter">quadcopter</a> design has become popular, though this layout is rarely used for crewed aircraft. Miniaturization means that less-powerful propulsion technologies can be used that are not feasible for crewed aircraft, such as small electric motors and batteries.
</p><p>Control systems for UAVs are often different from crewed craft. For remote human control, a camera and video link almost always replace the cockpit windows; radio-transmitted digital commands replace physical cockpit controls. <a href="/wiki/Autopilot" title="Autopilot">Autopilot</a> software is used on both crewed and uncrewed aircraft, with varying feature sets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Aircraft_configuration">Aircraft configuration</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Aircraft configuration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>UAVs can be designed in different configurations than manned aircraft, both because there is no need for a cockpit and its windows, and there is no need to optimize for human comfort, although some UAVs are adapted from piloted examples, or are designed for optionally piloted modes. <a href="/wiki/Air_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Air safety">Air safety</a> is also less of a critical requirement for unmanned aircraft, allowing the designer greater freedom to experiment. Instead, UAVs are typically designed around their onboard payloads and their ground equipment. These factors have led to a great variety of airframe and motor configurations in UAVs.
</p><p>For conventional flight, the <a href="/wiki/Flying_wing" title="Flying wing">flying wing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blended_wing_body" title="Blended wing body">blended wing body</a> offer light weight combined with low <a href="/wiki/Drag_(aerodynamics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Drag (aerodynamics)">drag</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stealth_aircraft" title="Stealth aircraft">stealth</a>, and are popular configurations for many use cases. Larger types which carry a variable payload are more likely to feature a distinct <a href="/wiki/Fuselage" title="Fuselage">fuselage</a> with a tail for stability, control and trim, although the <a href="/wiki/Wing_configuration" title="Wing configuration">wing configurations</a> in use vary widely.
</p><p>For uses that require vertical flight or hovering, the tailless <a href="/wiki/Quadcopter" title="Quadcopter">quadcopter</a> requires a relatively simple control system and is common for smaller UAVs. <a href="/wiki/Multirotor" title="Multirotor">Multirotor</a> designs with 6 or more rotors is more common with larger UAVs, where redundancy is prioritized.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Propulsion">Propulsion</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unmanned_aerial_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Propulsion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
